class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><p>Cybercriminals will often use LNK files attached in an email to launch an attack on unsuspecting victims. And we recently noticed another campaign using this technique.</p>

<p>In May 2020, we observed several LNK files in the wild, which we attribute to the same threat actor based on the code overlap, similar tactics, techniques and procedures (TTPs) and similar backdoor. For those who are unfamiliar, an LNK file is a shortcut or &quot;link&quot; used by Windows as a reference to an original file, folder, or application similar to an alias on the Macintosh platform.</p>

<p>The final backdoor, to the best of our knowledge, has not been documented before in the public domain. Recently, Malwarebytes published a <a href="https://blog.malwarebytes.com/threat-analysis/2020/06/higaisa/">blog</a> about this attack, but the details of the backdoor were not mentioned in that blog. This backdoor uses sophisticated and deceptive techniques, such as FakeTLS-based network communication over a duplicated socket handle and a complex cryptographic key derivation routine.</p>

<p>We attribute this attack (with a moderate confidence level) to the South Korean advanced persistent threat (APT) actor Higaisa. The decoy files used in the two instances of the LNK attack targeted users of Chinese origin.</p>

<p>The infection chain used by the LNK files is very similar to the instance observed in March 2020 by <a href="https://www.anomali.com/blog/covid-19-themes-are-being-utilized-by-threat-actors-of-varying-sophistication">Anomali</a>. The C&amp;C network infrastructure was correlated to Higaisa APT.</p>

<p>In this blog, we provide a detailed description of the distribution strategy, threat attribution, shellcode, anti-analysis techniques and the final backdoor of this campaign.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2>Distribution strategy</h2>

<p>The LNK files used by this threat actor contain decoy files that are displayed to the user while the malicious activities are carried out in the background. The decoy content could be an internet shortcut file (.url file extension) or a PDF file. In this section, we will describe the various themes used in this campaign.</p>

<p>On May 12, 2020, we discovered two LNK files that used the Zeplin platform (zeplin.io) as the decoy theme. Zeplin is a collaboration platform used by developers and designers in the enterprise industry. The details of the LNK files include:</p>

<p><strong>MD5 hash</strong>: 45278d4ad4e0f4a891ec99283df153c3</p>

<p><strong>Filename</strong>: Conversations - iOS - Swipe Icons - Zeplin.lnk</p>

<p><strong>MD5 hash</strong>: c657e04141252e39b9fa75489f6320f5</p>

<p><strong>Filename</strong>: Tokbox icon - Odds and Ends - iOS - Zeplin.lnk</p>

<p>These LNK files contain internet shortcut files that will be opened by the web browser installed on the system.</p>

<p>The URLs correspond to a project as shown below:</p>

<p>Project URL for file with MD5 hash: 45278d4ad4e0f4a891ec99283df153c3</p>

<p><a href="https://app.zeplin.io/project/5b5741802f3131c3a63057a4/screen/5b589f697e44cee37e0e61df">https://app.zeplin.io/project/5b5741802f3131c3a63057a4/screen/5b589f697e44cee37e0e61df</a></p>

<p>Project URL for file with MD5 hash: c657e04141252e39b9fa75489f6320f5</p>

<p><a href="https://app.zeplin.io/project/5b5741802f3131c3a63057a4/screen/5b589f697e44cee37e0e61df">https://app.zeplin.io/project/5b5741802f3131c3a63057a4/screen/5b589f697e44cee37e0e61df</a></p>

<p>If the user is not logged into the site, apps.zeplin.io, then it will redirect the user to the login page as shown in Figure 1.</p>

<p> </p>

<p><img alt="apps.zeplin.io login page" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/apps.zeplin.io%20login%20page.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 1: The login page displayed by Zeplin.</em></p>

<p><strong>MD5 hash of RAR archive</strong>: 2ffb817ff7ddcfa216da31f50e199df1</p>

<p><strong>Filename</strong>: Project link and New copyright policy.rar</p>

<p>The contents of the RAR archive are shown below:</p>

<p> </p>

<p>├── Project link and New copyright policy</p>

<p>│   ├── All tort&#x27;s projects - Web lnks</p>

<p>│   │   ├── Conversations - iOS - Swipe Icons - Zeplin.lnk</p>

<p>│   │   └── Tokbox icon - Odds and Ends - iOS - Zeplin.lnk</p>

<p>│   └── Zeplin Copyright Policy.pdf</p>

<p> </p>

<p>The contents of the decoy PDF are related to Zeplin’s copyright policy as shown in Figure 2.</p>

<p><em>Figure 2: The decoy PDF displaying Zeplin’s copyright policy notice.</em></p>

<p><strong>MD5 hash</strong>: 4a4a223893c67b9d34392670002d58d7</p>

<p><strong>Filename</strong>:</p>

<p>Curriculum Vitae_WANG LEI_Hong Kong Polytechnic University.pdf.lnk</p>

<p>This LNK file drops a PDF file at runtime and opens it with the default PDF viewer on the system.</p>

<p><strong>MD5 hash of the dropped PDF file</strong>: 4dcd2e0287e0292a1ad71cbfdf99726e</p>

<p><strong>Filename of decoy PDF</strong>: Curriculum Vitae_WANG LEI_Hong Kong Polytechnic University.pdf</p>

<h2> </h2>

<h2>LNK metadata analysis</h2>

<p>The LNK file format contains a wealth of metadata information that can be used for attribution and correlating the files to a particular threat actor. While most of the metadata from the LNK files in this attack was erased, we found the Security Identifier (SID) value preserved in the LNK files.</p>

<p>Using the LECmd tool, we extracted the SID value from the LNK files which are detailed in the table below:</p>

<p> </p>

<table><tbody><tr><td>
			<p><strong>LNK file MD5 hash</strong></p>
			</td><td>
			<p><strong>SID value</strong></p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>997ab0b59d865c4bd63cc55b5e9c8b48</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>S-1-5-21-1624688396-48173410-756317185-1001</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>c657e04141252e39b9fa75489f6320f5</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>S-1-5-21-1624688396-48173410-756317185-1001</p>
			</td></tr><tr><td>
			<p>4a4a223893c67b9d34392670002d58d7</p>
			</td><td>
			<p>S-1-5-21-1624688396-48173410-756317185-1001</p>

<p> </p>

<p>We wrote a YARA hunting rule to discover other LNK files in the wild with the same SID value as shown below:</p>

<p>rule ZS_LNK_SID</p>

<p>{</p>

<p>            strings:</p>

<p>                                    $a = &quot;S-1-5-21-1624688396-48173410-756317185-1001&quot; wide</p>

<p>            condition:</p>

<p>                                    $a</p>

<p>}</p>

<p>The only instances we found were the above four LNK files. So, in addition to other indicators shared between these four LNK files, the common SID values helped us to further attribute them to the same threat actor.</p>

<h2> </h2>

<h2>Technical analysis</h2>

<p>For the purpose of technical analysis, we will use the LNK file with MD5 hash: 45278d4ad4e0f4a891ec99283df153c3.</p>

<p>If the Chrome browser is already installed on the machine, then the icon of the LNK file will appear to be the same as the Chrome browser icon. This is because the IconFileName property in the LNK file is set to the path of the Chrome browser as shown below:</p>

<p>IconFileName - C:\Program Files (x86)\Google\Chrome\Application\chrome.exe</p>

<p>The target property of the LNK file specifies the command that will be executed at runtime as shown in Figure 5.</p>

<p><img alt="LNK command line" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/lnk_command_line.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 5: The LNK command target.</em></p>

<p>This command starts the infection chain and involves multiple stages as detailed below :</p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
	<li>Copies the original LNK file to the temporary directory in the location: %temp%\g4ZokyumB2DC.tmp</li>
	<li>Iterates over the files in the C:\Windows\System32 directory to search for certutil.exe</li>
	<li>Copies certutil.exe to %temp%\gosia.exe</li>
	<li>Uses findstr.exe to search for the marker “TVNDRgA” inside the original LNK file.</li>
	<li>Using the market, a base64 encoded blob is extracted to the temporary file: %temp%\cSi1rouy.tmp</li>
	<li>Uses certutil.exe to decode the base64 encoded blob to the file: %temp%\o423DFDS.tmp</li>
	<li>The resulting decoded file has the CAB file format.</li>
	<li>Uses expand.exe to extract the contents of the CAB file to the %temp% directory.</li>
</ul>

<p>The components of the cab file are shown in Figure 6.</p>

<p><img alt="CAB file contents" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/cab_file_contents.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 6: The CAB file contents.</em></p>

<p>Here is a brief description of each component of the CAB file. They are described in more details later in the blog.</p>

<p><strong>3t54dE3r.tmp</strong> – Contains the shellcode that will be loaded and executed at runtime.</p>

<p><strong>34fDFkfSD32.js</strong> – The JavaScript that is used to initiate the infection chain after extraction of CAB file contents.</p>

<p><strong>Conversations - iOS - Swipe Icons – Zeplin.url</strong> – This is the internet shortcut file that will be used to open the URL: https://app.zeplin.io/project/5b5741802f3131c3a63057a4/screen/5b589f697e44cee37e0e61df with Chrome browser on the machine.</p>

<p><strong>Svchast.exe</strong> – This is the shellcode loader binary that spoofs the name of a legitimate Windows binary called svchost.exe. Other details include:</p>

<ul>
	<li>The LNK file will open the internet shortcut file (which opens by default with the web browser and loads the URL).</li>
	<li>It copies the CAB file component, 3t54dE3r.tmp to the location: C:\Users\Public\Downloads\3t54dE3r.tmp</li>
	<li>It uses wscript.exe to execute the JavaScript file: 34fDFkfSD32.js</li>
</ul>

<h2>JavaScript file analysis</h2>

<p><strong>MD5 hash of the JavaScript file</strong>: a140420e12b68c872fe687967ac5ddbe</p>

<p>The contents of the JavaScript are shown in Figure 7.</p>

<p><img alt="JavaScript file contents" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/JavaScript%20file.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 7: The JavaScript file contents</em></p>

<p>Below are the main operations performed by this JavaScript file.</p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
	<li>It runs the ipconfig command to gather information about the machine&#x27;s network adapter configuration. It then redirects the results of this command to the file: C:\\Users\\Public\\Downloads\\d3reEW.txt</li>
	<li>It copies svchast.exe to the Startup directory in the location: %AppData%\\Microsoft\\Windows\\Start Menu\\Programs\\Startup\\officeupdate.exe for persistence:</li>
	<li>It copies svchast.exe to the location: C:\\Users\\Public\\Downloads\\officeupdate.exe</li>
	<li>It uses schtasks.exe to create a scheduled task with the name: “Driver Bootser Update” which will be used to execute the officeupdate.exe binary</li>
	<li>It executes svchast.exe binary.</li>
	<li>It sends an HTTP POST request to the URL: <a href="http://zeplin.atwebpages.com/inter.php">hxxp://zeplin.atwebpages.com/inter.php</a> and exfiltrates the ipconfig output gathered from the machine.</li>
</ul>

<h2>Shellcode loader analysis</h2>

<p><strong>MD5 hash</strong>: a29408dbedf1e5071993dca4a9266f5c</p>

<p><strong>Filename</strong>: svchast.exe</p>

<p>The file svchast.exe is used to load the shellcode stored in the file 66DF3DFG.tmp in the path: C:\Users\Public\Downloads\66DF3DFG.tmp</p>

<p>This path is hardcoded in the loader.</p>

<p>The shellcode is loaded using the following steps:</p>

<ol>
	<li>It reads the contents of the file, “C:\Users\Public\Downloads\66DF3DFG.tmp” into a newly allocated memory region marked with PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE permission.</li>
	<li>It transfers the control to this memory region to start the execution of the shellcode.</li>
</ol>

<h2>Shellcode analysis</h2>

<p>In this section, we have detailed the interesting code sections of the shellcode.</p>

<p><strong>Anti-debugging technique</strong></p>

<p>The shellcode uses an anti-debugging technique to calculate a 32-bit hash of the code section. This is done to detect the presence of any software breakpoints or tampering of code done for the purpose of reverse engineering.</p>

<p>When a software breakpoint is added in the debugger, a byte with the value 0xCC is added by the debugger in place of the original operation code (opcode). As a result of this, the hash calculation is corrupted.</p>

<p>Such anti-debugging techniques can be easily bypassed by using hardware breakpoints instead of software breakpoints.</p>

<p>As an example, let us set a software breakpoint at the comparison instruction right after hash calculation and check the resulting hash calculated (shown in Figure 8).</p>

<p><img alt="Software Breakpoint" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/software%20breakpoint.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 8: The software breakpoint detection by anti-debugging techniques in the shellcode.</em></p>

<p>As can be seen in Figure 8, due to the software breakpoint, the computed hash was corrupted. Because of this, the code can detect the presence of a debugger. The shellcode will exit the execution if it detects a debugger.</p>

<p>However, if we set a hardware breakpoint, the computed hash will be correct as shown in Figure 9.</p>

<p><img alt="Hardware breakpoint" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/hardware%20breakpoint.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 9: The hardware breakpoint bypasses the anti-debugging technique in the shellcode.</em></p>

<p>We re-wrote the algorithm used by the shellcode to calculate the hash of the code section in Python and it can be found in <strong>Appendix I</strong>.</p>

<p><strong>Decryption of data in the buffer</strong></p>

<p>The shellcode uses a 16-byte XOR key for decrypting the data as shown in Figure 10.</p>

<p><img alt="decryption of data in buffer" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/decryption%20of%20data%20in%20buffer.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 10: Decryption of the data in the buffer. XOR decryption used to decrypt the strings.</em></p>

<p>The 16-byte XOR key used for decryption is:</p>

<p>key = [0xE4, 0xFD, 0x23, 0x99, 0xA3, 0xE1, 0xD3, 0x58, 0xA6, 0xCC, 0xDB, 0xE8, 0xF2, 0x91, 0xD2, 0xF8]</p>

<p>We re-wrote the decryption code in Python and can been seen in <strong>Appendix II</strong>.</p>

<p>Since we believe this to be a new backdoor, we have shared the complete list of decrypted strings in <strong>Appendix IV</strong> for reference.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2><strong>Key generation routine</strong></h2>

<p>In the first thread created by the shellcode, it generates a cryptographic session key that will be transmitted later to the C&amp;C server to protect the communication channel between the bot and the server.</p>

<p>In this section, we detail the key generation routine.</p>

<p>There are multiple parts that are concatenated together to form the final key.</p>

<p><strong>Part 1:</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
	<li>It calls UUIDCreate() API to generate a UUID.</li>
	<li>It uses the format string: “%08X....-%04X...-%0llX” to format the UUID using sprintf().</li>
</ul>

<p>Example UUID: DB7C6235-FD1A-45B6-224F868</p>

<p><strong>Part 2:</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
	<li>It calls UUIDCreate() to generate a 16-byte UUID.</li>
	<li>The last byte of the UUID is used to generate a byte that will be used to perform the ROR operation later.</li>
	<li>It uses an ROR and ADD instruction-based algorithm to compute a 32-bit hash that will be appended to first two steps (listed above). The algorithm used to compute the 32-bit hash in this case is similar to the one used in the anti-debugging section. This algorithm has been re-written in Python and can be found in <strong>Appendix I</strong>.</li>
</ul>

<p>Format:</p>

<p>uuid2 = [] [ROR byte 0x00 0x00 0x00] [32-bit hash]</p>

<ul>
	<li>It uses CryptBinaryToStringA() to generate Base64 encoded data using UUID2.</li>
</ul>

<p><strong>Part 3:</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
	<li>It uses Windows Crypto APIs to generate an MD5 hash using UUID1 (from Part 1). Before the hash is calculated, the length of the UUID is extended to 0x48 bytes by padding with null bytes. This can be re-written in Python as:</li>
</ul>

<p>data = uuid1 + “\x00” * (0x48 - len(uuid1))</p>

<p>md5 = hashlib.md5()</p>

<p>md5.update(data)</p>

<p>hash1 = md5.hexdigest()</p>

<ul>
	<li>It calculates an MD5 hash of the above-generated hash once again.</li>
</ul>

<p>hash2 = md5(hash1)</p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
	<li>It uses CryptDeriveKey() to derive a 128-bit AES key.</li>
</ul>

<p><img alt="CryptDeriveKey" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/cryptderivekey.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 11: The cryptographic session key derivation routine.</em></p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
	<li>It appends hash2 with null bytes to extend the length to 0x48 bytes and then encrypts it using the AES-128 bit key derived in step 3 above. The encrypted hash is used to derive the AES key for encryption.</li>
</ul>

<p>All these parts are concatenated together before transmitting to the C&amp;C server for registering the AES key for encrypted communication.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2><strong>Initialization of a TLS session</strong></h2>

<p>After decrypting the C&amp;C server address, the shellcode proceeds to send an HTTP GET request to fetch the resource: “msdn.cpp” on the server.</p>

<p>WinHTTPSetOption() is used to set the WINHTTP_OPTION_SECURITY_FLAGS value to 0x3300, which allows it to ignore any certificate errors that might occur at the time of the request.</p>

<p>Figure 12 shows that the content-length request header field in the HTTP GET request is set to: 0xffffffff manually at the time of invoking the WinHTTPSendRequest.</p>

<p><img alt="Initial Network Request sent to the server" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/network_request.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 12: The initial request sent to the C&amp;C server for deception purposes to make it look like a TLS session</em></p>

<p>The HTTP GET request looks like:</p>

<p>GET hxxps://45.76.6[.]149/msdn.cpp HTTP/1.1<br/>
Connection: Keep-Alive<br/>
User-Agent: WinHTTP/1.1<br/>
Content-Length: 4294967295 
Host: 45.76.6[.]149</p>

<p>This HTTP GET request was sent for deception purposes to make it look like a valid TLS session. As we will see later, a FakeTLS session is used by the shellcode to perform C&amp;C communication with the server.<br/>
 </p>

<h2><strong>Duplication of socket - ShadowMove similarity</strong></h2>

<p>We discovered an interesting code section in this shellcode which creates a duplicate socket to connect to the C2 server. The method is very similar to the ShadowMove lateral movement technique which was presented in <a href="https://www.usenix.org/system/files/sec20summer_niakanlahiji_prepub.pdf">Usenix 2020</a>.</p>

<p>At first glance, due to the high level of code overlap in this shellcode with the above technique, we believed it to be using the ShadowMove lateral movement technique. However on further inspection, we concluded that this technique was used to create a duplicate socket that will be used for FakeTLS communication as described in the next section.</p>

<p>Below are the details of the steps used by the shellcode to create a duplicate socket used for communication with the C2 server:</p>

<p> </p>

<ul>
	<li>It calls the NtQuerySystemInformation() native API with the InfoClass parameter set to: SystemExtendedHandleInformation (0x40). This fetches detailed information for all the handles and their corresponding object names.</li>
	<li>The information is returned in the form of a SYSTEM_HANDLE_TABLE_ENTRY_INFO_EX structure.</li>
	<li>It uses a GetCurrentProcessID to find the process ID of the current process.</li>
	<li>It compares the UniqueProcessID member of the SYSTEM_HANDLE_TABLE_ENTRY_INFO_EX structure with the current process ID. If they are equal, then it proceeds to the next step.</li>
	<li>It compares the HandleValue member of the SYSTEM_HANDLE_TABLE_ENTRY_INFO_EX structure with the socket handle. If they are equal, then it proceeds to the next step.</li>
	<li>It creates a new thread that calls the native API, NtQueryObject() to retrieve information about the object. The information is returned in the structure: __PUBLIC_OBJECT_TYPE_INFORMATION.</li>
	<li>If the TypeName member of the structure __PUBLIC_OBJECT_TYPE_INFORMATION is equal to “\Device\Afd”, then it proceeds to the next step. It is important to note that Windows sockets have the object type “\Device\Afd”.</li>
	<li>It calls getpeername() to get the IP address and port number corresponding to the above socket.</li>
	<li>It compares the IP address and port number with the expected values corresponding to the C&amp;C server.</li>
	<li>If the correct socket is found, then it calls DuplicateHandle() to duplicate this socket.</li>
</ul>

<p>Figure 13 shows the code section that locates the socket handle.</p>

<p><img alt="Shadow Move 1" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/shadow_move_1.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 13: The subroutine that is used to iterate over system handles.</em></p>

<p>Figure 14 shows the code section that checks if the socket handle corresponds to the socket used to communicate with the C&amp;C server.</p>

<p><img alt="Shadow Move 2" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/shadow_move_2.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 14: The subroutine that used to locate the target socket handle used to communicate with the C&amp;C server.</em></p>

<p> </p>

<h2><strong>FakeTLS</strong></h2>

<p>We observed interesting use of the FakeTLS method in this shellcode. It creates a FakeTLS header using the byte sequence: [0x17 0x03 0x01] as shown in Figure 15.</p>

<p><img alt="FakeTLS" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/fakeTLS.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 15: The subroutine used to craft the FakeTLS header.</em></p>

<p>It is important to note that this FakeTLS method has been used in the past by APT groups, such as Lazarus.</p>

<p>The reason for using this technique is to confuse network monitoring security systems that do not perform proper SSL inspection and, as a result, allow the traffic to pass through.</p>

<p>Also, we noticed two requests sent by the bot using the FakeTLS header in the initialization phase.</p>

<p><strong>Request 1 [Fake session key]</strong></p>

<p>In the first request, the routine:</p>

<ul>
	<li>Uses time() to get the current time.</li>
	<li>Uses srand() to seed the pseudo-random number generator using the value obtained in step 1.</li>
	<li>Uses rand() to generate a random number.</li>
	<li>Generates a total of 0xC3 random bytes using the above method.</li>
	<li>Appends a total of 0x3C bytes with the value 0xAD to the data generated in step 4.</li>
</ul>

<p>So a total of 0xFF bytes are generated in the format: [0xC3 bytes of random data][0x3C bytes with value 0xAD].</p>

<p>This data is appended to the FakeTLS header and sent using ws2_32.send() to the C&amp;C server as shown in Figure 16.</p>

<p><img alt="FakeTLS Fqke Key" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/fakeTLS_fake_key.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 16: The FakeTLS packet appended with random data.</em></p>

<p>It is important to note that this memory chunk is freed using VirtualFree() after sending it in a request to the C&amp;C server. So we do not believe this was used as a session key because, in that case, the bot would have to preserve the key somewhere.</p>

<p><strong>Request 2 [Real session key]</strong></p>

<p>In the second instance of the request sent to the C&amp;C server, we noticed the FakeTLS header appended with the cryptographic session key generated earlier as shown in Figure 17.</p>

<p><img alt="FakeTLS Real Key" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/fakeTLS_real_key.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 17: FakeTLS header appended with cryptographic session key.</em></p>

<p>The data appended to the FakeTLS header has the following format:</p>

<p>[command padded to 4 bytes][size padded to 4 bytes][base64-encoded data from Part2][Hash2 - padded to 0x48 bytes][AES-128 bit Encrypted Key].</p>

<p>Below is an example of a packet with the FakeTLS Header and the data appended after it. The structure of the packet is detailed in Figure 18.</p>

<p><img alt="FakeTLS Packet Structure" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/TLS%20packet%20structure.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 18: The packet structure containing the FakeTLS header and custom format used for C&amp;C communication.</em></p>

<p>Other messages contain encrypted data right after the TLS header.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2>C&amp;C communication</h2>

<p>The shellcode creates two more threads that work together to handle the commands exchanged between the backdoor and the C&amp;C server.</p>

<p>Below are the main steps used by the C&amp;C command handler:</p>

<ul>
	<li>IT creates a dispatch thread that will handle the commands posted to it by the worker thread.</li>
	<li>The dispatch thread creates a message queue using the PeekMessageW() API.</li>
	<li>The worker thread sends the message ID along with the command buffer to the message queue using PostThreadMessageW() API.</li>
	<li>Once a message is posted to the dispatch thread by the worker thread, it is retrieved using the GetMessageW() API. This message will be dispatched to the appropriate command handler based on the ID of the message as detailed below.</li>
</ul>

<p>There are two sets of command IDs. One of them corresponds to commands from client to server and the other set corresponds to commands from server to client. Corresponding to each command, there is a size of the command.</p>

<p>As an example,</p>

<p>Client to server: The command ID 0x65 corresponds to the backdoor registering the system ID (calculated using UUID) with the C&amp;C server and the cryptographic session key as shown in Figure 18 above.</p>

<p>Server to client: The command ID 0x64 is used to receive the encryption key that will be used by the client to encrypt the data sent to the server.</p>

<p>At the time of analysis, since the C2 server was not responding, we cannot conclusively determine the commands that were supported by this backdoor.</p>

<p> </p>

<h2>Zscaler Cloud Sandbox detection</h2>

<p>Figure 19 shows the Zscaler Cloud Sandbox successfully detecting this LNK-based threat.</p>

<p><img alt="Cloud Sandbox Detection" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/cloud%20sandbox%20detection.png" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 19: The Zscaler Cloud Sandbox detection.</em></p>

<p>In addition to sandbox detections, Zscaler’s multilayered cloud security platform detects indicators at various levels:</p>

<p><a href="https://threatlibrary.zscaler.com/threats/afedef5b-2164-4ae1-8f2d-7fde74d556b4/">L</a><a href="https://threatlibrary.zscaler.com/?keyword=LNK.Dropper.Higaisa">NK.Dropper.Higaisa</a></p>

<p> </p>

<h2>Conclusion</h2>

<p>This new instance of attack from the Higaisa APT group shows that they are actively updating their tactics, techniques and procedures (TTPs) and incorporating new backdoors with evasion techniques. The network communication protocol between the backdoor and the C&amp;C server is deceptive and complex, which was designed to evade network security solutions.</p>

<p>Users are advised to take extra precaution while opening LNK files sent inside email attachments. LNK files can have the file icon of legitimate applications, such as Web browsers or PDF reader applications, so the source of the files should be verified before opening them.</p>

<p>The Zscaler ThreatLabZ team will continue to monitor this campaign, as well as others, to help keep our customers safe.</p>

<p> </p>

<p><strong>Appendix I</strong></p>

<p><strong>Anti-debugging hash computation</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<p># Hash of code section before decryption should be equal to 0x733C7595</p>

<p># Hash of code section after decryption should be equal to 0x6621A914</p>

<p> </p>

<p># read the shellcode contents</p>

<p>contents = open(“shellcode.bin”, “rb”).read()</p>

<p> </p>

<p># x86 ROR instruction re-written in Python</p>

<p>ror = lambda val, r_bits, max_bits: \</p>

<p>            ((val &amp; (2**max_bits-1)) &gt;&gt; r_bits%max_bits) | \</p>

<p>            (val 

</p><p> </p>

<p># x86 movsx instruction re-written in Python</p>

<p>def SIGNEXT(x, b):</p>

<p>            m = 1 

</p><p>            x = x &amp; ((1 

</p><p>            return (x ^ m) - m</p>

<p> </p>

<p># limit = length of code section used for hash calculation</p>

<p># First 0xcb06 bytes are used to calculate the hash</p>

<p> </p>

<p>for i in range(0xcb06):</p>

<p>            result = ror(result, 0xa, 32)</p>

<p>            t = SIGNEXT(ord(contents[i]), 8) &amp; 0xffffffff</p>

<p>            result += t</p>

<p>            result = result &amp; 0xffffffff</p>

<p> </p>

<p>print “final hash is: %x” %(result)</p>

<p> </p>

<p><strong>Appendix II</strong></p>

<p><strong>XOR decryption code to extract plaintext strings and C&amp;C server address</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<p>import binascii, struct, sys</p>

<p> </p>

<p># read the contents of shellcode</p>

<p>contents = open(sys.argv[1], &quot;rb&quot;).read()</p>

<p> </p>

<p># XOR decrypt the strings</p>

<p>def decrypt_data(encrypted, key):</p>

<p>decrypt = &quot;&quot;</p>

<p>for i in range(len(encrypted)):</p>

<p>db = encrypted[i]</p>

<p>kb = key[i % len(key)]</p>

<p> </p>

<p>if(type(kb) == type(&quot;&quot;)):</p>

<p>kb = ord(kb)</p>

<p>if(type(db) == type(&quot;&quot;)):</p>

<p>db = ord(db)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>decrypt += chr(db ^ kb)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>return decrypt</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def extract_c2(contents):</p>

<p>key = contents[0xcb0e:0xcb1e]</p>

<p>encrypted = contents[0xcb1e:]</p>

<p>decrypt = &quot;&quot;</p>

<p>decrypt = decrypt_data(encrypted, key)</p>

<p>return &quot;{}:{}&quot;.format(decrypt[432:].split(&quot;\x00&quot;)[0],struct.unpack(&quot;<h>

<p><br/>
 </p>

<p>print(&quot;==C2 Server==\n{}\n&quot;.format(extract_c2(contents)))</p>

<p> </p>

<p># Encrypted data is present at offset, 0xacc0 and has a total length of 0x12b0</p>

<p>encrypted = contents[0xacc0:0xacc0+0x12b0]</p>

<p> </p>

<p>#16-byte XOR key</p>

<p>key = [0xE4, 0xFD, 0x23, 0x99, 0xA3, 0xE1, 0xD3, 0x58, 0xA6, 0xCC, 0xDB, 0xE8, 0xF2, 0x91, 0xD2, 0xF8]</p>

<p>print(&quot;==Strings==&quot;)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>for item in decrypt_data(encrypted, key).split(&quot;\x00&quot;):</p>

<p>if item:</p>

<p>print(item)</p>

<p> </p>

<p><strong>Appendix III</strong></p>

<p><strong>Script to generate AES key message</strong></p>

<p>from wincrypto import CryptCreateHash, CryptHashData, CryptDeriveKey, CryptEncrypt, CryptImportKey, CryptExportKey,  CryptGetHashParam, CryptDecrypt</p>

<p>from wincrypto.constants import CALG_SHA1, CALG_AES_256, bType_SIMPLEBLOB, CALG_AES_128, CALG_MD5</p>

<p>import binascii, base64, struct, uuid</p>

<p> </p>

<p>###  Hash functions ###</p>

<p>ror = lambda val, r_bits, max_bits: \</p>

<p>   ((val &amp; (2**max_bits-1)) &gt;&gt; r_bits%max_bits) | \</p>

<p>   (val 

</p><p> </p>

<p># x86 movsx instruction re-written in Python</p>

<p>def SIGNEXT(x, b):</p>

<p>   m = 1 

</p><p>   x = x &amp; ((1 

</p><p>   return (x ^ m) - m</p>

<p> </p>

<p>def get_hash(uuid1):</p>

<p>   result = 0</p>

<p>   for i in range(len(uuid1)):</p>

<p>       result = ror(result, 0xa, 32)</p>

<p>       t = SIGNEXT(uuid1[i], 8) &amp; 0xffffffff</p>

<p>       result += t</p>

<p>       result = result &amp; 0xffffffff</p>

<p>   return result</p>

<p> </p>

<p> </p>

<p>### UUID convert from bytes to base64 ###</p>

<p>uuid0 = uuid.uuid4().bytes</p>

<p>uuid0_wh = uuid0 + b&quot;\x00\x00\x00&quot;  + struct.pack(&quot;<i of="" uuuid1="">

</i></p><p>uuid0_enc = base64.b64encode(uuid0_wh) + b&quot;\x0d\x0a&quot; #append &quot;\r\n&quot; added by windows API</p>

<p> </p>

<p>### Derive key from UUID ####</p>

<p>#Generate uuid</p>

<p>uuid1 = str(uuid.uuid4())</p>

<p>#Append NULL bytes to make length equal to 0x48</p>

<p>data = uuid1 + (b&quot;\x00&quot; * (0x48 - len(uuid1)))</p>

<p>#Generate MD5 hash</p>

<p>hasher = CryptCreateHash(CALG_MD5)</p>

<p>CryptHashData(hasher, data)</p>

<p>uuid1_md5 = CryptGetHashParam(hasher,0x2)</p>

<p>#Append NULL bytes to md5 and again generate md5 hash to make length equal to 0x48</p>

<p>uuid1_md5_md5 = uuid1_md5 + (b&quot;\x00&quot; * (0x48 - len(uuid1_md5)))</p>

<p>hasher = CryptCreateHash(CALG_MD5)</p>

<p>CryptHashData(hasher, uuid1_md5_md5)</p>

<p>#Derive AES key</p>

<p>aes_key = CryptDeriveKey(hasher, CALG_AES_128)</p>

<p>#Encrypt Send MD5 hash using AES</p>

<p>encrypted_hash = CryptEncrypt(aes_key, uuid1_md5_md5)</p>

<p>#append more NULL bytes to Encrypted hash to make length 0x90</p>

<p>encrypted_hash_padded = encrypted_hash + (b&quot;\x00&quot; * (0x90 - len(encrypted_hash)))</p>

<p>#Again use encrypted hash to calculate its md5 and derive new AES key</p>

<p>hasher = CryptCreateHash(CALG_MD5)</p>

<p>CryptHashData(hasher, encrypted_hash_padded)</p>

<p>aes_key = CryptDeriveKey(hasher, CALG_AES_128)</p>

<p> </p>

<p>#generate message buffer to send to server to register key</p>

<p>fake_tls_header =  b&quot;\x17\x03\x01&quot;</p>

<p>client_key_message_header = b&quot;\x65\x00\x00\x00\xd8\x00\x00\x00&quot;</p>

<p>buffer = client_key_message_header +  uuid0_enc + b&quot;\x00\x00&quot; + uuid1_md5_md5 + encrypted_hash_padded</p>

<p>buffer = fake_tls_header + struct.pack(&quot;&gt;h&quot;, len(buffer))  + buffer</p>

<p> </p>

<p>binascii.hexlify(buffer)</p>

<p>len(buffer)</p>

<p> </p>

<p><strong>Appendix V</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<p><strong>Structure of packet containing AES key</strong></p>

<p> </p>

<p>struct Packet {</p>

<p>            struct FakeTls {</p>

<p>            struct AppDataHeader{</p>

<p>                                     byte tls_header_app_data_constant;</p>

<p>             byte tls_version_major;</p>

<p>                                     byte tls_version_minor;</p>

<p>            } tls_app_data_header  ;</p>

<p>                        ushort  PacketSize;</p>

<p>            } FakeTlsHeader ;</p>

<p> </p>

<p>            struct PacketData {</p>

<p>                        int  Command ; //(0x65 Client to Server 0x64 Server to Client) AES key</p>

<p>                        int  DataSize ;</p>

<p>                        char SystemId[0x22];</p>

<p>                        char Padding[2];</p>

<p>                        byte data[DataSize] ;</p>

<p>            } command ;</p>

<p> </p>

<p>} packet;</p>
</h></p>

will often use LNK files attached in an email to launch an attack on unsuspecting victims. And we recently noticed another campaign using this technique.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn May 2020, we observed several LNK files in the wild, which we attribute to the same threat actor based on the code overlap, similar tactics, techniques and procedures (TTPs) and similar backdoor. For those who are unfamiliar, an LNK file is a shortcut or \"link\" used by Windows as a reference to an original file, folder, or application similar to an alias on the Macintosh platform.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe final backdoor, to the best of our knowledge, has not been documented before in the public domain. Recently, Malwarebytes published a \u003ca href=\"https://blog.malwarebytes.com/threat-analysis/2020/06/higaisa/\"\u003eblog\u003c/a\u003e about this attack, but the details of the backdoor were not mentioned in that blog. This backdoor uses sophisticated and deceptive techniques, such as FakeTLS-based network communication over a duplicated socket handle and a complex cryptographic key derivation routine.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe attribute this attack (with a moderate confidence level) to the South Korean advanced persistent threat (APT) actor Higaisa. The decoy files used in the two instances of the LNK attack targeted users of Chinese origin.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe infection chain used by the LNK files is very similar to the instance observed in March 2020 by \u003ca href=\"https://www.anomali.com/blog/covid-19-themes-are-being-utilized-by-threat-actors-of-varying-sophistication\"\u003eAnomali\u003c/a\u003e. The C\u0026amp;C network infrastructure was correlated to Higaisa APT.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn this blog, we provide a detailed description of the distribution strategy, threat attribution, shellcode, anti-analysis techniques and the final backdoor of this campaign.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eDistribution strategy\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe LNK files used by this threat actor contain decoy files that are displayed to the user while the malicious activities are carried out in the background. The decoy content could be an internet shortcut file (.url file extension) or a PDF file. In this section, we will describe the various themes used in this campaign.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eOn May 12, 2020, we discovered two LNK files that used the Zeplin platform (zeplin.io) as the decoy theme. Zeplin is a collaboration platform used by developers and designers in the enterprise industry. The details of the LNK files include:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMD5 hash\u003c/strong\u003e: While most of the metadata from the LNK files in this attack was erased, we found the Security Identifier (SID) value preserved in the LNK files.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eUsing the LECmd tool, we extracted the SID value from the LNK files which are detailed in the table below:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ctable\u003e\r\n\t\u003ctbody\u003e\r\n\t\t\u003ctr\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003ctd\u003e\r\n\t\t\t\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLNK file ZS_LNK_SID\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e{\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e            strings:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e                                    $a = So, in addition to other indicators shared between these four LNK files, the common SID values helped us to further attribute them to the same threat actor.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003e \u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eTechnical analysis\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFor the purpose of technical analysis, we will use the LNK file with MD5 hash: 45278d4ad4e0f4a891ec99283df153c3.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIf the Chrome browser is already installed on the machine, then the icon of the LNK file will appear to be the same as the Chrome browser icon. This is because the IconFileName property in the LNK file is set to the path of the Chrome browser as shown below:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIconFileName - C:\\Program Files (x86)\\Google\\Chrome\\Application\\chrome.exe\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe target property of the LNK file specifies the command that will be executed at runtime as shown in Figure 5.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"LNK command line\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/lnk_command_line.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 5: The LNK command target.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis command starts the infection chain and involves multiple stages as detailed below :\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eCopies the original LNK file to the temporary directory in the location: %temp%\\g4ZokyumB2DC.tmp\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIterates over the files in the C:\\Windows\\System32 directory to search for certutil.exe\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eCopies certutil.exe to %temp%\\gosia.exe\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eUses findstr.exe to search for the marker “TVNDRgA” inside the original LNK file.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eUsing the market, a base64 encoded blob is extracted to the temporary file: %temp%\\cSi1rouy.tmp\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eUses certutil.exe to decode the base64 encoded blob to the file: %temp%\\o423DFDS.tmp\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe resulting decoded file has the CAB file format.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eUses expand.exe to extract the contents of the CAB file to the %temp% directory.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe components of the cab file are shown in Figure 6.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"CAB file contents\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/cab_file_contents.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 6: The CAB file contents.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eHere is a brief description of each component of the CAB file. They are described in more details later in the blog.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e3t54dE3r.tmp\u003c/strong\u003e – Contains the shellcode that will be loaded and executed at runtime.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e34fDFkfSD32.js\u003c/strong\u003e – The JavaScript that is used to initiate the infection chain after extraction of CAB file contents.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConversations - iOS - Swipe Icons – Zeplin.url\u003c/strong\u003e – This is the internet shortcut file that will be used to open the URL: https://app.zeplin.io/project/5b5741802f3131c3a63057a4/screen/5b589f697e44cee37e0e61df with Chrome browser on the machine.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSvchast.exe\u003c/strong\u003e – This is the shellcode loader binary that spoofs the name of a legitimate Windows binary called svchost.exe. Other details include:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe LNK file will open the internet shortcut file (which opens by default with the web browser and loads the URL).\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt copies the CAB file component, 3t54dE3r.tmp to the location: C:\\Users\\Public\\Downloads\\3t54dE3r.tmp\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt uses wscript.exe to execute the JavaScript file: 34fDFkfSD32.js\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eJavaScript file analysis\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMD5 hash of the JavaScript file\u003c/strong\u003e: a140420e12b68c872fe687967ac5ddbe\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe contents of the JavaScript are shown in Figure 7.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"JavaScript file contents\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/JavaScript%20file.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 7: The JavaScript file contents\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBelow are the main operations performed by this JavaScript file.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt runs the ipconfig command to gather information about the machine's network adapter configuration. It then redirects the results of this command to the file: C:\\\\Users\\\\Public\\\\Downloads\\\\d3reEW.txt\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt copies svchast.exe to the Startup directory in the location: %AppData%\\\\Microsoft\\\\Windows\\\\Start Menu\\\\Programs\\\\Startup\\\\officeupdate.exe for persistence:\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt copies svchast.exe to the location: C:\\\\Users\\\\Public\\\\Downloads\\\\officeupdate.exe\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt uses schtasks.exe to create a scheduled task with the name: “Driver Bootser Update” which will be used to execute the officeupdate.exe binary\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt executes svchast.exe binary.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt sends an HTTP POST request to the URL: \u003ca href=\"http://zeplin.atwebpages.com/inter.php\"\u003ehxxp://zeplin.atwebpages.com/inter.php\u003c/a\u003e and exfiltrates the ipconfig output gathered from the machine.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eShellcode loader analysis\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMD5 hash\u003c/strong\u003e: a29408dbedf1e5071993dca4a9266f5c\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFilename\u003c/strong\u003e: svchast.exe\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe file svchast.exe is used to load the shellcode stored in the file 66DF3DFG.tmp in the path: C:\\Users\\Public\\Downloads\\66DF3DFG.tmp\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis path is hardcoded in the loader.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe shellcode is loaded using the following steps:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003col\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt reads the contents of the file, “C:\\Users\\Public\\Downloads\\66DF3DFG.tmp” into a newly allocated memory region marked with PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE permission.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt transfers the control to this memory region to start the execution of the shellcode.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ol\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eShellcode analysis\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn this section, we have detailed the interesting code sections of the shellcode.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAnti-debugging technique\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe shellcode uses an anti-debugging technique to calculate a 32-bit hash of the code section. This is done to detect the presence of any software breakpoints or tampering of code done for the purpose of reverse engineering.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWhen a software breakpoint is added in the debugger, a byte with the value 0xCC is added by the debugger in place of the original operation code (opcode). As a result of this, the hash calculation is corrupted.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSuch anti-debugging techniques can be easily bypassed by using hardware breakpoints instead of software breakpoints.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAs an example, let us set a software breakpoint at the comparison instruction right after hash calculation and check the resulting hash calculated (shown in Figure 8).\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Software Breakpoint\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/software%20breakpoint.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 8: The software breakpoint detection by anti-debugging techniques in the shellcode.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAs can be seen in Figure 8, due to the software breakpoint, the computed hash was corrupted. Because of this, the code can detect the presence of a debugger. The shellcode will exit the execution if it detects a debugger.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eHowever, if we set a hardware breakpoint, the computed hash will be correct as shown in Figure 9.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Hardware breakpoint\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/hardware%20breakpoint.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 9: The hardware breakpoint bypasses the anti-debugging technique in the shellcode.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe re-wrote the algorithm used by the shellcode to calculate the hash of the code section in Python and it can be found in \u003cstrong\u003eAppendix I\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDecryption of data in the buffer\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe shellcode uses a 16-byte XOR key for decrypting the data as shown in Figure 10.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"decryption of data in buffer\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/decryption%20of%20data%20in%20buffer.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 10: Decryption of the data in the buffer. XOR decryption used to decrypt the strings.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe 16-byte XOR key used for decryption is:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003ekey = [0xE4, 0xFD, 0x23, 0x99, 0xA3, 0xE1, 0xD3, 0x58, 0xA6, 0xCC, 0xDB, 0xE8, 0xF2, 0x91, 0xD2, 0xF8]\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe re-wrote the decryption code in Python and can been seen in \u003cstrong\u003eAppendix II\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSince we believe this to be a new backdoor, we have shared the complete list of decrypted strings in \u003cstrong\u003eAppendix IV\u003c/strong\u003e for reference.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eKey generation routine\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn the first thread created by the shellcode, it generates a cryptographic session key that will be transmitted later to the C\u0026amp;C server to protect the communication channel between the bot and the server.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn this section, we detail the key generation routine.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThere are multiple parts that are concatenated together to form the final key.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePart 1:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt calls UUIDCreate() API to generate a UUID.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt uses the format string: “%08X....-%04X...-%0llX” to format the UUID using sprintf().\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eExample UUID: DB7C6235-FD1A-45B6-224F868\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePart 2:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt calls UUIDCreate() to generate a 16-byte UUID.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe last byte of the UUID is used to generate a byte that will be used to perform the ROR operation later.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt uses an ROR and ADD instruction-based algorithm to compute a 32-bit hash that will be appended to first two steps (listed above). The algorithm used to compute the 32-bit hash in this case is similar to the one used in the anti-debugging section. This algorithm has been re-written in Python and can be found in \u003cstrong\u003eAppendix I\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFormat:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003euuid2 = [] [ROR byte 0x00 0x00 0x00] [32-bit hash]\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt uses CryptBinaryToStringA() to generate Base64 encoded data using UUID2.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePart 3:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt uses Windows Crypto APIs to generate an MD5 hash using UUID1 (from Part 1). Before the hash is calculated, the length of the UUID is extended to 0x48 bytes by padding with null bytes. This can be re-written in Python as:\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003edata = uuid1 + “\\x00” * (0x48 - len(uuid1))\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003emd5 = hashlib.md5()\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003emd5.update(data)\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003ehash1 = md5.hexdigest()\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt calculates an MD5 hash of the above-generated hash once again.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003ehash2 = md5(hash1)\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt uses CryptDeriveKey() to derive a 128-bit AES key.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"CryptDeriveKey\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/cryptderivekey.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 11: The cryptographic session key derivation routine.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt appends hash2 with null bytes to extend the length to 0x48 bytes and then encrypts it using the AES-128 bit key derived in step 3 above. The encrypted hash is used to derive the AES key for encryption.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAll these parts are concatenated together before transmitting to the C\u0026amp;C server for registering the AES key for encrypted communication.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eInitialization of a TLS session\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAfter decrypting the C\u0026amp;C server address, the shellcode proceeds to send an HTTP GET request to fetch the resource: “msdn.cpp” on the server.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWinHTTPSetOption() is used to set the WINHTTP_OPTION_SECURITY_FLAGS value to 0x3300, which allows it to ignore any certificate errors that might occur at the time of the request.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 12 shows that the content-length request header field in the HTTP GET request is set to: 0xffffffff manually at the time of invoking the WinHTTPSendRequest.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Initial Network Request sent to the server\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/network_request.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 12: The initial request sent to the C\u0026amp;C server for deception purposes to make it look like a TLS session\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe HTTP GET request looks like:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eGET hxxps://45.76.6[.]149/msdn.cpp HTTP/1.1\u003cbr\u003e\r\nConnection: Keep-Alive\u003cbr\u003e\r\nUser-Agent: WinHTTP/1.1\u003cbr\u003e\r\nContent-Length: 4294967295 \r\nHost: 45.76.6[.]149\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis HTTP GET request was sent for deception purposes to make it look like a valid TLS session. As we will see later, a FakeTLS session is used by the shellcode to perform C\u0026amp;C communication with the server.\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDuplication of socket - ShadowMove similarity\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe discovered an interesting code section in this shellcode which creates a duplicate socket to connect to the C2 server. The method is very similar to the ShadowMove lateral movement technique which was presented in \u003ca href=\"https://www.usenix.org/system/files/sec20summer_niakanlahiji_prepub.pdf\"\u003eUsenix 2020\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAt first glance, due to the high level of code overlap in this shellcode with the above technique, we believed it to be using the ShadowMove lateral movement technique. However on further inspection, we concluded that this technique was used to create a duplicate socket that will be used for FakeTLS communication as described in the next section.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBelow are the details of the steps used by the shellcode to create a duplicate socket used for communication with the C2 server:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt calls the NtQuerySystemInformation() native API with the InfoClass parameter set to: SystemExtendedHandleInformation (0x40). This fetches detailed information for all the handles and their corresponding object names.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe information is returned in the form of a SYSTEM_HANDLE_TABLE_ENTRY_INFO_EX structure.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt uses a GetCurrentProcessID to find the process ID of the current process.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt compares the UniqueProcessID member of the SYSTEM_HANDLE_TABLE_ENTRY_INFO_EX structure with the current process ID. If they are equal, then it proceeds to the next step.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt compares the HandleValue member of the SYSTEM_HANDLE_TABLE_ENTRY_INFO_EX structure with the socket handle. If they are equal, then it proceeds to the next step.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt creates a new thread that calls the native API, NtQueryObject() to retrieve information about the object. The information is returned in the structure: __PUBLIC_OBJECT_TYPE_INFORMATION.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIf the TypeName member of the structure __PUBLIC_OBJECT_TYPE_INFORMATION is equal to “\\Device\\Afd”, then it proceeds to the next step. It is important to note that Windows sockets have the object type “\\Device\\Afd”.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt calls getpeername() to get the IP address and port number corresponding to the above socket.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIt compares the IP address and port number with the expected values corresponding to the C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIf the correct socket is found, then it calls DuplicateHandle() to duplicate this socket.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 13 shows the code section that locates the socket handle.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Shadow Move 1\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/shadow_move_1.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 13: The subroutine that is used to iterate over system handles.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 14 shows the code section that checks if the socket handle corresponds to the socket used to communicate with the C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Shadow Move 2\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/shadow_move_2.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 14: The subroutine that used to locate the target socket handle used to communicate with the C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFakeTLS\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe observed interesting use of the FakeTLS method in this shellcode. It creates a FakeTLS header using the byte sequence: [0x17 0x03 0x01] as shown in Figure 15.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"FakeTLS\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/fakeTLS.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 15: The subroutine used to craft the FakeTLS header.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt is important to note that this FakeTLS method has been used in the past by APT groups, such as Lazarus.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe reason for using this technique is to confuse network monitoring security systems that do not perform proper SSL inspection and, as a result, allow the traffic to pass through.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAlso, we noticed two requests sent by the bot using the FakeTLS header in the initialization phase.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRequest 1 [Fake session key]\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn the first request, the routine:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eUses time() to get the current time.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eUses srand() to seed the pseudo-random number generator using the value obtained in step 1.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eUses rand() to generate a random number.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eGenerates a total of 0xC3 random bytes using the above method.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eAppends a total of 0x3C bytes with the value 0xAD to the data generated in step 4.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSo a total of 0xFF bytes are generated in the format: [0xC3 bytes of random data][0x3C bytes with value 0xAD].\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis data is appended to the FakeTLS header and sent using ws2_32.send() to the C\u0026amp;C server as shown in Figure 16.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"FakeTLS Fqke Key\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/fakeTLS_fake_key.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 16: The FakeTLS packet appended with random data.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt is important to note that this memory chunk is freed using VirtualFree() after sending it in a request to the C\u0026amp;C server. So we do not believe this was used as a session key because, in that case, the bot would have to preserve the key somewhere.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRequest 2 [Real session key]\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn the second instance of the request sent to the C\u0026amp;C server, we noticed the FakeTLS header appended with the cryptographic session key generated earlier as shown in Figure 17.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"FakeTLS Real Key\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/fakeTLS_real_key.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 17: FakeTLS header appended with cryptographic session key.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe data appended to the FakeTLS header has the following format:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e[command padded to 4 bytes][size padded to 4 bytes][base64-encoded data from Part2][Hash2 - padded to 0x48 bytes][AES-128 bit Encrypted Key].\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBelow is an example of a packet with the FakeTLS Header and the data appended after it. The structure of the packet is detailed in Figure 18.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"FakeTLS Packet Structure\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/TLS%20packet%20structure.png\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 18: The packet structure containing the FakeTLS header and custom format used for C\u0026amp;C communication.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eOther messages contain encrypted data right after the TLS header.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eC\u0026amp;C communication\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe shellcode creates two more threads that work together to handle the commands exchanged between the backdoor and the C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBelow are the main steps used by the C\u0026amp;C command handler:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eIT creates a dispatch thread that will handle the commands posted to it by the worker thread.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe dispatch thread creates a message queue using the PeekMessageW() API.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eThe worker thread sends the message ID along with the command buffer to the message queue using PostThreadMessageW() API.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003eOnce a message is posted to the dispatch thread by the worker thread, it is retrieved using the GetMessageW() API. This message will be dispatched to the appropriate command handler based on the ID of the message as detailed below.\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThere are two sets of command IDs. One of them corresponds to commands from client to server and the other set corresponds to commands from server to client. Corresponding to each command, there is a size of the command.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAs an example,\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eClient to server: The command ID 0x65 corresponds to the backdoor registering the system ID (calculated using UUID) with the C\u0026amp;C server and the cryptographic session key as shown in Figure 18 above.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eServer to client: The command ID 0x64 is used to receive the encryption key that will be used by the client to encrypt the data sent to the server.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAt the time of analysis, since the C2 server was not responding, we cannot conclusively determine the commands that were supported by this backdoor.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eZscaler Cloud Sandbox detection\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 19 shows the Zscaler Cloud Sandbox successfully detecting this LNK-based threat.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Cloud Sandbox Detection\" data-entity-type=\"\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Higaisa/cloud%20sandbox%20detection.png\" loading=\"lazy\"
